<div id="review_860351751" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/860351751" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/860351751?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140449570" href="/book/show/326768.Dangerous_Liaisons">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=best-villains">best-villains</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=metafiction">metafiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=rth-lifetime">rth-lifetime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=hot-sex">hot-sex</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=top-100">top-100</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer860351751" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9658574661481327332">The 18th century is a tough nut to crack. Its <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143085414' title='Pamela: boring and shitty' rel='nofollow noopener'>most</a> <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139736763' title='Tom Jones: pleasant but boring' rel='nofollow noopener'>famous</a> <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143096610' title='Tristram Shandy: tricky and brilliant but also often boring' rel='nofollow noopener'>books</a> are boring. It's an explosively smutty era, but even most of the smut <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/132179264' title='Fanny Hill: literature's greatest game of PENIS' rel='nofollow noopener'>isn't</a> <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/141321124' title='Diderot: semi-hot nun on nun action' rel='nofollow noopener'>that</a> <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/186415694' title='The thing with de Sade is that he's not very bright' rel='nofollow noopener'>great.</a> But there are a few weird gems that slip through the cracks: the furious <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139717065' title='The best of all possible books' rel='nofollow noopener'>Candide;</a> the sensational <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145984037' title='Sin ahoy' rel='nofollow noopener'>Monk;</a> and the masterpiece of smut Dangerous Liaisons.<br/><br/>Epistolaries were big back then, and LaClos makes better use of letters than anyone since <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91666684' title='sucks to be Rosencrantz or Guildenstern' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shakespeare;</a> it'll take <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/190831963' title='Friggin' Fosco, right?' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wilkie Collins</a> to match him. The letters <i>are</i> the plot, making this metafiction; the</span>
  <span id="freeText9658574661481327332" style="display:none">The 18th century is a tough nut to crack. Its <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143085414" title="Pamela: boring and shitty" rel="nofollow noopener">most</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139736763" title="Tom Jones: pleasant but boring" rel="nofollow noopener">famous</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143096610" title="Tristram Shandy: tricky and brilliant but also often boring" rel="nofollow noopener">books</a> are boring. It's an explosively smutty era, but even most of the smut <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/132179264" title="Fanny Hill: literature's greatest game of PENIS" rel="nofollow noopener">isn't</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/141321124" title="Diderot: semi-hot nun on nun action" rel="nofollow noopener">that</a> <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/186415694" title="The thing with de Sade is that he's not very bright" rel="nofollow noopener">great.</a> But there are a few weird gems that slip through the cracks: the furious <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/139717065" title="The best of all possible books" rel="nofollow noopener">Candide;</a> the sensational <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145984037" title="Sin ahoy" rel="nofollow noopener">Monk;</a> and the masterpiece of smut Dangerous Liaisons.<br /><br />Epistolaries were big back then, and LaClos makes better use of letters than anyone since <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/91666684" title="sucks to be Rosencrantz or Guildenstern" rel="nofollow noopener">Shakespeare;</a> it'll take <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/190831963" title="Friggin' Fosco, right?" rel="nofollow noopener">Wilkie Collins</a> to match him. The letters <i>are</i> the plot, making this metafiction; their content and their incriminating existence shape and drive the action. This is the best advertisement for Snapchat I've ever seen.<br /><br />It's known as an immoral novel, and it was banned almost immediately and permanently, and you could think of it as an anti-<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143085414" title="My review again" rel="nofollow noopener">Pamela:</a> where those letters were supposed to be a guide to a virtuous life, these are a master class in corruption. <br /><br />The filth is one reason it's fun, but the reason it's great is its terrific character insight. Valmont and, most of all, the inimitable Merteuil are perfectly, subtly, carefully drawn; their <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[tragic <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> arcs clearly laid out and never escapable. They say a lot on paper; they say more between the lines. You root for all of them. Even the <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <s>casualties</s>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> minor characters are fully fleshed out and sympathetic. It's bizarre that LaClos only wrote one book; he seems perfectly in control of every sentence. This is a page-turner, a thriller, a gamechanger, and one of my favorite books. <br /><br /><b>Translation notes:</b> Helen Constantine's recentish one for Penguin got good reviews in my research, and I totally loved it. The voices are distinct; the language is readable without being distractingly modern. The introduction is more or less total bullshit.<br /><br /><b>Adaptations:</b> I just re-watched Cruel Intentions last night and it's still good trashy fun, but it doesn't do a very good job of adapting the book. The biggest problem is Valmont: the movie plays him too sympathetically, and Ryan Philippe is not at all good enough for the role. And it flubs the ending. Sarah Michelle Gellar is adequate. Selma Blair seems to be acting in a different movie - a broad slapstick comedy - but it's entertaining, and Cecile isn't taken very seriously in the book either so that works out fine. Reese Witherspoon is good but her breasts walk away with every scene they're in. That's one of the all-time great cinematic portrayals of breasts. <br /><br />It's been a while since I've seen the 80s Dangerous Liaisons with Glenn Close, Malkovich and Uma Thurman; I remember it being really good but weren't the former two like way, way too old for those roles? Both were around 40.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9658574661481327332" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_345752693" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/345752693" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/345752693?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

          marked it as dropped


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140116373" href="/book/show/180710.Les_Liaisons_Dangereuses">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer345752693" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12560147796534109149">If I were the sort of boner who ran a creative writing night class I might level that grievous accusation at this Gallic favourite—how it “tells” everything and doesn’t “show.” And if you were a frightfully witty sort, you may reply: “Duh. It’s written in letters.” And such a Daria-strength comeback would be entirely appropriate: this <i>is</i> an epistolary novel where effusive aristocrats compose long-winded letters about their schemes and feelings and dire circumstances, with little for the reader t</span>
  <span id="freeText12560147796534109149" style="display:none">If I were the sort of boner who ran a creative writing night class I might level that grievous accusation at this Gallic favourite—how it “tells” everything and doesn’t “show.” And if you were a frightfully witty sort, you may reply: “Duh. It’s written in letters.” And such a Daria-strength comeback would be entirely appropriate: this <i>is</i> an epistolary novel where effusive aristocrats compose long-winded letters about their schemes and feelings and dire circumstances, with little for the reader to cling to except the inevitable moral corruptions as promised in the blurb and the flashy period prose. I slightly recoil. The epistolary form is so frightfully dull. Regardless how many naïve virgins may lose their maidenhead to unscrupulous bounders. This stream of melodramatic back-and-forth plot-explaining missives lacked any real narrative drive for me, as bitchily funny as the two corrupt lovers were on occasion. I got enough from 100pp or so. Enough for me. You don’t need to finish <i>everything</i> you read, right? I mean I don’t get all obsessive about these things. (Er . . . much). And I suppose you won’t ever catch me reading the unabridged <i>Clarissa</i> (according to this novel’s preface the characters in <i>Clarissa</i> would have to have spent eight hours per day composing their letters for the timeframe to work). Yowza. Cheeses. Wow-wee. Etcetera. By the way the movie version of this is terrific, featuring the hammy delights of a youngish John Malkovich and the nubile breasts of a younger Uma Thurman. Perfick.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12560147796534109149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I learned how to look cheerful while under the table I stuck a fork onto the back of my hand. I became a virtuoso of deceit. I consulted the strictest moralists to learn how to appear, philosophers to find out what to think, and novelist to see what I could get away with, and in the end it all came down to one wonderfully simple principle: win or die.”</i>

<div id="review_1557715495" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1557715495" />
    <a title="Emma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28602001-emma"><img alt="Emma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1438967931p2/28602001.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1557715495?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emma" href="/user/show/28602001-emma">Emma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/28856114-les-liaisons-dangereuses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28602001-emma?shelf=audible">audible</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1557715495" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9166714342090940854">What a deliciously wicked story.<br/><br/>This dramatisation from the cast of the Donmar production is worth listening to for Janet McTeer alone. Her Marquise de Merteuil was incredible. Sensual, scathing, scandalous. If you are new to Laclos, this is a great place to start. I read an English translation many years ago and loved it, but this version brought it to life for me. It has left me wanting to read the original, and i'll be purchasing it after finishing the review (yes, i'm THAT enthused).<br/><br/>It seems</span>
  <span id="freeText9166714342090940854" style="display:none">What a deliciously wicked story.<br /><br />This dramatisation from the cast of the Donmar production is worth listening to for Janet McTeer alone. Her Marquise de Merteuil was incredible. Sensual, scathing, scandalous. If you are new to Laclos, this is a great place to start. I read an English translation many years ago and loved it, but this version brought it to life for me. It has left me wanting to read the original, and i'll be purchasing it after finishing the review (yes, i'm THAT enthused).<br /><br />It seems to me that Laclos' work has a real modern relevance. We live in a society in which a person is still judged on their sexual self. Whether that be the sexual preference they claim, how they go about their romantic/sexual lives, or how their sexuality is displayed to others. People still talk of 'conquest' over another, if not always using that specific word, the idea is the same. We have online sites for showcasing clips of sexual acts unknowingly filmed, for revenge porn, for rating the attractiveness and 'easiness' of others. 'Slut' and 'whore' are judgment terms that still have real power. Perhaps the men reading this will correct me if i'm wrong, but I don't think there are words used with the same derision for male behaviour, even 'manwhore' and 'male slut' are not without positive connotations, part of male banter. This has been my experience of male-female heterosexual relations, and I wonder how these themes are played out within the homosexual or trans communities. Without a doubt, external, societal judgment is still evident. <br /><br />For this reason, the most significant character has always been, for me, La Marquise de Merteuil. Valmont, the rake, the seducer, the ruiner of women is a male character so often done, before and since, as to be stereotypical. La Marquise, on the other hand, demands to be his equal, or even better: in manipulation, in deceit, in pursuit of pleasure. She makes precisely this point. His machinations, his successes, are all the more easy because of his gender, and the resulting freedoms it accords him. <br /><br />In any case, I love this format in literature. Private letters dispatched at cross purposes allows for an intriguing and amusing insight into character; what they say to one person and hide from another, virtuous and indignant here, debauched and desirous there. An excellent reflection of the multitude of selves we all hold.<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="9166714342090940854" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=movie-fodder">movie-fodder</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=mandatory-reading">mandatory-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=best-bad-guys">best-bad-guys</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=stole-it-from-mom">stole-it-from-mom</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1353346930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1286080055034311732">A literary tour-de-force, this book is a magnificent, perverse story of manipulation, seduction, betrayal and deceit. Published a few years before the French Revolution, Laclos allegedly meant it as a slap to the face of the decadent aristocracy, their abuse of position and power, their immoral and depraved conduct and hypocrisy.<br/><br/>Told in a clever epistolary format, this is the story of an intrigue instigated by the bored Marquise de Merteuil; a former lover, the Comte de Gercourt is to marry a yo</span>
  <span id="freeText1286080055034311732" style="display:none">A literary tour-de-force, this book is a magnificent, perverse story of manipulation, seduction, betrayal and deceit. Published a few years before the French Revolution, Laclos allegedly meant it as a slap to the face of the decadent aristocracy, their abuse of position and power, their immoral and depraved conduct and hypocrisy.<br /><br />Told in a clever epistolary format, this is the story of an intrigue instigated by the bored Marquise de Merteuil; a former lover, the Comte de Gercourt is to marry a young and virtuous girl, Cécile Volanges. The Marquise wants to humiliate Gercourt by corrupting his innocent bride-to-be with the help of another former lover, the Vicomte de Valmont - a notorious Casanova. But Valmont has another scheme in mind: he wishes to push a pious married woman, Madame de Tourvel, into betraying her husband by becoming his lover. The Marquise de Merteuil then hatches a plan that would kill two birds with one stone... The two schemers proceed to artfully pull the strings of each other's vanity and ego, and turn their lives into an intricate game of chess where every piece/person is a pawn with the exception of the King and Queen.<br /><br />This is obviously as juicy and convoluted as stories gets, but Laclos' genius lies not in creating the first soap opera: it lies in the brilliance of his characterization. Valmont and Merteuil are cynical, libertine and decadent, but through the numerous letters that weave the story, we come to know them as extremely complex creations. Their correspondance is delightfully twisted and it is a guilty pleasure to read those letters and laugh as they recall the sordid affairs they were both involved with in the past, but this cold and calculating behaviour has its roots in the constraining social structure that they must live in. Their cruel amusements soon looses their edge to become a horrifying symptom of a much deeper an subtler suffering that the one those seducers inflict on others.<br /><br />A mind-blowing passage illustrates that the Marquise de Merteuil - one of the most impressive villains of French literature - fuels her rotten passions with the frustration of being a woman with a man's character and appetites in a man's world. To command the same respect as a man would in her position, while still slacking her lusts, she needs to be much stronger, much more implacable than any man in her situation would have to be… and it pisses her off. She presents a front of kindness and virtue as she distills a stronger venom than any man, who's social position would never be endangered by their libertine daliances. She is by far the most fascinating character of the book. To be honest, I ended up feeling sad for her: being smarter and stronger than the men in the room has never been easy. Neither has the act of perpetually wearing a mask to retain one's position and privileges. And we must remember that the Marquise would not have half the freedom she enjoys had she not been a widow to begin with…<br /><br />Valmont and Cécile are also interesting to examine. The first is a selfish seducer who gets caught at his own game for the first time in his life and who has no defense against something he always considered himself immune to. He is a fiend, but he is also free to live his life as he chooses with very little consequences, and his over-confidence eventually becomes his demise. As for Cécile, she does start out innocent and inexperienced, but the Marquise makes a very astute point in noticing that she has the sort of character that yearns for intrigue and adventure and only needs to be exposed to it to get a taste for it. She doesn't have the capacity to understand what the long-term repercussions of her budding tastes are, which is where this game becomes dangerous for her.<br /><br />It is also interesting to think of the context in which it was written: Laclos was considered almost as scandalous a writer as his contemporary the Marquis de Sade, who wrote books that were openly mocking what he considered to be the naive Rousseau-ist optimism pedeled by the philosophers of the day. He wanted to remind people that human nature is not intrinsically good and generous, that people are as capable of terrible things as they are capable of admirable ones. While Laclos had noble patrons, he scorches them mercilessly, and his satyre seems to have been so successful that queen Marie-Antoinette herself apparently enjoyed the book without ever noting the irony…<br /><br />To the modern reader, morally corrupt characters like those found in these pages are nothing new, but we still live in a world where we can be judged on our sexual history, and a sexist double-standard is still applied; in the age of revenge porn, "Dangerous Liaisons" remains an important, and brilliantly written, story. I admire Laclos for putting this issue at the center of his masterpiece.<br /><br />The movie adaptation starring Glenn Close and John Malkovich is visually stunning and very strong, but it nevertheless pales when compared to the original work. Of course, one must be willing to deal with the slightly archaic turn of phrase and expressions, but the reward of how Laclos made each voice so distinct and their banter so sharp and darkly funny makes up for the slower and more tedious passages. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1286080055034311732" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/497510659?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer497510659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1189966113390631348">This is one of the most intruiging early novels (end of the 18th C.). The construction of the story is utterly sublime, as an epistolary novel with letters going back and forth between the Marquise de Merteuil and the Vicomte de Valmont (both amoral, narcistic personalities), full of mirror effects and hidden parallels.<br/><br/>The originality of this work is that this is one of the first novels in history in which words and language are used in a very perverse way (it set the mark for thousands of other</span>
  <span id="freeText1189966113390631348" style="display:none">This is one of the most intruiging early novels (end of the 18th C.). The construction of the story is utterly sublime, as an epistolary novel with letters going back and forth between the Marquise de Merteuil and the Vicomte de Valmont (both amoral, narcistic personalities), full of mirror effects and hidden parallels.<br /><br />The originality of this work is that this is one of the first novels in history in which words and language are used in a very perverse way (it set the mark for thousands of others), inviting the reader to permanently search for the hidden meaning behind what is written. Instead of what most people think, the real focus of the story is not on seduction, but on power. And not the character of the libertarist count Valmont is new in literature, but that of the vilain marquise de Merteuil, making the novel - again in very perverse way - a forerunner in emancipated literature. I read this in the original French version, which of course adds to the exquisiteness of the novel.<br /><br />Addendum: one of the best female Dutch authors of the 20th Century, Hella S. Haasse, has written a beautiful sequel on this novel, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1248747.Een_gevaarlijke_verhouding_of_Daal_en_Bergse_brieven" title="Een gevaarlijke verhouding of Daal-en-Bergse brieven by Hella S. Haasse" rel="nofollow noopener">Een gevaarlijke verhouding of Daal-en-Bergse brieven</a>. She uses the perspective of the marquise de Merteuil, living her last days in The Hague, and musing on the female condition. Very recommended, although unfortunately it has only been translated in French, not in English. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1189966113390631348" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText7301564337593119200" style="display:none">I wonder, if I had read this book when I was 21 instead of 31, would I have saved myself a good deal of grief concerning relationships? This book masterfully exposes every kind of grief there is. But, I think that like the innocent characters in the book, I wouldn't have understood it at the time. <br /><br />When attempting to navigate love, one always messes up somewhere. Some of us stomp around like... a yeti, lol. While others are deft and cruel. Toss both these sorts of people together into a restrictive society, and voila! Dangerous Liaisons. <br /><br />Frankly, this books does what "Men are from Mars, Women are from Venus" sets out to do, but with plenty of (oh so true) warnings that make you want to run screaming from such games. <br /><br />Perhaps that's why I liked it so much.<br /><br />The author, Choderlos de Laclos has actually written an essay about the state of women's education. I'd love to read it, but can't find it anywhere. From what I understand, he was pretty harsh on how society kept women in ignorance. Rock on de Laclos. </span>

<div id="review_3415364341" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3415364341" />
    <a title="Luís" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s"><img alt="Luís" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598708286p2/117399210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3415364341?book_show_action=true">Jun 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Luís" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Luís" href="/user/show/117399210-lu-s">Luís</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782253004011" href="/book/show/22017690-les-liaisons-dangereuses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=gallimard-and-related">gallimard-and-related</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=french-literature">french-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/117399210-lu-s?shelf=romance">romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3415364341" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12689153539756803364">I've had and highly appreciated the reading of the Dangerous Liaisons. The opportunity presented itself to discover more about this author that is Pierre Choderlos de Laclos.<br/>"Women and their education" consists in fact of three texts, written independently, unfinished, but grouped by their common subject: the woman.<br/>The first text is the outline of a provocative speech in response to a question posed by the Châlons-sur-Marne Academy: "What would be the best ways to improve women's education?" His</span>
  <span id="freeText12689153539756803364" style="display:none">I've had and highly appreciated the reading of the Dangerous Liaisons. The opportunity presented itself to discover more about this author that is Pierre Choderlos de Laclos.<br />"Women and their education" consists in fact of three texts, written independently, unfinished, but grouped by their common subject: the woman.<br />The first text is the outline of a provocative speech in response to a question posed by the Châlons-sur-Marne Academy: "What would be the best ways to improve women's education?" His answer is clear: no. Considering that woman is subject to men, to their laws, to society, she is in a certain way her slave. There can be no education if there is slavery. I appreciated with eloquence his position in favour of women in this speech.<br />The following text is a 12-chapter essay in which the author describes and values the "natural" human being, focusing in particular on the "natural" woman (in opposition to the "social" woman) to a revolution of morals. He draws heavily on Rousseau's theses concerning the naturally good aspect of man, unfortunately, perverted by society.<br />I found interesting his position on the relationship which he must remain privileged between the mother and her child. Whether it is she who takes care of it and nourishes it from her breast, and not a nurse as was the case, for the majority of noble families at the time.<br />It also advises women, who can lend a smile, about their lifestyle and the amenities they must have to maintain their "natural" beauty.<br />The last text is devoted to reading which he considers crucial and essential in the education of a young woman. It gives an overview of what it should read and in what order: it establishes a real program with proposals of titles and authors! It even suggests methods for integrating these readings. It is for women, but her advice is for both sexes, in my opinion.<br />A man was disconcerting that this M. Laclos! Spouse and good father of family, career soldier, then an active member of the Jacobins, he spent some time in prison for his links with Danton, then he will approach Napoleon. I miss a lot because he made a more than an exciting journey, having lived and participated in critical moments in the history of France. And in parallel, in his lost hours I dare say, he finds the time and energy to be a poet, a philosopher and to give us a gift of the masterpiece of the "Dangerous Liaisons". Hats off Mr Choderlos de Laclos!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12689153539756803364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_406613" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/406613" />
    <a title="Mikey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24801-mikey"><img alt="Mikey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1173742079p2/24801.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/406613?book_show_action=true">Mar 24, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mikey" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mikey" href="/user/show/24801-mikey">Mikey</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140441161" href="/book/show/326770.Les_Liaisons_Dangereuses">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/24801-mikey?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer406613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7619412356278761873">Aside from the fact that <i>Les Liaisons Dangereuses</i> has a tight, efficient plot and well-constructed characters, what's most impressive about it is how well it works as an actual <i>epistolary</i> novel. Instead of Clarissa writing for 18 hours a day, what we have hear are short (1-2 pages, sometimes less) letters, of the length that people might actually write to one another, and multiple correspondences, in order to keep the story fresh and told from multiple perspectives. In addition, the letters beco</span>
  <span id="freeText7619412356278761873" style="display:none">Aside from the fact that <i>Les Liaisons Dangereuses</i> has a tight, efficient plot and well-constructed characters, what's most impressive about it is how well it works as an actual <i>epistolary</i> novel. Instead of Clarissa writing for 18 hours a day, what we have hear are short (1-2 pages, sometimes less) letters, of the length that people might actually write to one another, and multiple correspondences, in order to keep the story fresh and told from multiple perspectives. In addition, the letters become part of the story itself; letters turn up as pieces of evidence against certain characters, etc. This is the novel that inspired the films <i>Dangerous Liaisons</i>, <i>Valmont</i>, and <i>Cruel Intentions</i>, and it's very bit as saucy and nasty as those movies. Indeed, the dialog (in letter form) is among the most clever you'll ever find in a novel. This is one of my all-time favorites.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7619412356278761873" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2249060429" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2249060429" />
    <a title="Fede" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73964263-fede"><img alt="Fede" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604333859p2/73964263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2249060429?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fede" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fede" href="/user/show/73964263-fede">Fede</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9782070338962" href="/book/show/1424484.Les_Liaisons_dangereuses">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=french">french</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2249060429" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1607777474217255594">Reading this epistolary novel is like having a panic attack in a mirror maze. <br/>You feel trapped, lost in the logic of a game so wicked and twisted that no player is ever supposed to win more than he loses. A cruel chess game in which the pieces are the players themselves - mercilessly, cold-bloodedly moving each other. <br/><br/>Paris, last decades of the 'ancien régime' and its magnificent, triumphant amorality. <br/>The Marquise de Merteuil is certainly not the kind of woman who cries over a lost lover. She i</span>
  <span id="freeText1607777474217255594" style="display:none">Reading this epistolary novel is like having a panic attack in a mirror maze. <br />You feel trapped, lost in the logic of a game so wicked and twisted that no player is ever supposed to win more than he loses. A cruel chess game in which the pieces are the players themselves - mercilessly, cold-bloodedly moving each other. <br /><br />Paris, last decades of the 'ancien régime' and its magnificent, triumphant amorality. <br />The Marquise de Merteuil is certainly not the kind of woman who cries over a lost lover. She is far from wallowing in black moods, jealousy, sentimentality, melancholy; she is far from love in the first place. What she cannot bear is to be forgotten. To watch her discarded lover get over her on account of a young girl freshly out of the convent, where noble girls are taught how to become devoted wives and impeccable ladies. As though the Marquise, a real woman who makes men wild with desire, could be put aside and leave her place to a dumb little girl without taking her revenge. <br />She does not waste her time indeed: she writes to her - uhm - intimate friend and confident, the Vicomte de Valmont, asking him to seduce the girl (Mademoiselle Cécile de Volanges) and openly corrupt her, so as to humiliate her lover once and for all by showing him how deceiving female virtue is. In the meantime, she is also going to seduce Cécile's beloved, Danceny - little more than a kid actually: just to take some personal advantage while Valmont enjoys himself. To keep herself busy, we might say.<br />As for Valmont, he is already playing his own cruel game with the Présidente de Tourvel, whose immaculate reputation is a challenge to his lust. He is not in love with her of course: what he is up to is the sheer destruction of her resistance and the satisfaction of his pride. In his own words: <br /><br />'It has become necessary for me to have this woman, so as to save myself from the ridicule of being in love with her: for to what lengths will a man not be driven by a thwarted desire?' <br /><br />Since all the characters have their own secret aim, often colliding with someone else's, the plot gets incredibly convoluted. The reader is soon overwhelmed by a stifling atmosphere, as though imprisoned in Merteuil's mind. It is like a mounting wave of claustrophobia. <br />What Merteuil and Valmont have set in motion is a depraved vicious circle, a self-devouring beast: things get out of control, nothing - and nobody - is what it seems anymore and tragedy will finally join forces with human ruthlessness to end all games. <br />At the end of this book even justice leaves a bad taste in your mouth. <br /><br />***<br /><br />In his premise Laclos states that his aim in collecting and publishing these (obviously not) authentic letters is to show how base, corrupted, even murderous the upper class of his time is. This moralizing attempt only apparently puts his book on the same level with the philosophical novels of the 18th century: the reader perceives the ironic undertones of this introduction. After all the book is published in 1782, when the Revolutionary fire is still little more than a sparkle; and we must keep in mind that Laclos is an amateur writer as well as a military officer, so that he himself is part of that same establishment: aristocracy, military commanders, high-ranking functionaries share the same pleasures and vices, live in the same gloomy boredom, commit the same crimes. His writing cannot be a real denunciation, least of all a condemnation of the status quo. What this novel does have in common with the Enlightment literature is not in its intent but in its form: the depiction of society - the privileged part of it - in all its ugliness and moral decline. We are not spared any outrageos detail. Laclos is a spectator watching a play through his binoculars: his vision is as clear and detached, cold and objective as Merteuil's or Valmont's reflections.<br />The writer does not really conceal his admiration for these ruthless puppeteers. These wicked children playing with human toys to quench their burning thirst of life. In fact they are the only intelligent individuals in the novel, moving among a crowd of morons and prigs and bigoted old spinsters. Merteuil's mind is brilliant precisely because she lacks any sort of moral restraint or 'female virtue'. Her intellect is never lead astray by feelings of love, faithfulness, tenderness, remorse, least of all compassion:<br /><br />'He'd call me false and faithless and I've always had a weakness for those two words; next to cruel, they're the nicest words for a woman to hear, and not so hard to earn'. <br /><br />Virtuous or depraved, all the characters are slowly but inexorably doomed by her revenge, a black hole suckin everybody in, and Laclos' theorem is finally proved by the events: the weapons of virtue are too weak to resist the fascination of sin. As de Sade will write a few years later in "The 120 Days of Sodom", <br /><br />'Nothing is more contagious than Evil'. <br /><br />Hard not to agree while reading these chilling letters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1607777474217255594" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText12369157208948623434" style="display:none">"Fragonard amoureux" Exhibition at Luxembourg Museum Paris.<br /><br />Everyone knows Fragonard and his gallant paintings. The exhibition is splendid. A firework of pleasure and sensuality. The force of Jean-Honore is that he never was vulgar. No pornography as we can see in others painter's work of this time. All is suggested, in particular with the pillows or thwarts. A remark of the conservative intrigued me. Starting from 1770, Jean-Honore gives up the libertinage as model for inspiration. Happiness is in the couple, in the search for harmony in love. The reason? this book. Problem : "Les liaisons" was published in 1782 and Jean-Honoré stop the painting in 1786. <br /><br />In fact, I think that the artists are sensitive to collect the spirit of the time before everyone. The end of the libertinage time was the end of an era. Then I read again this night "Les liaisons...". That give to me another vision of this book. <br />Valmont is the first romantic hero. <br /> </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1885937545" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4589997779755873">What a novel! Never have I come across one, as far as I can recall, that so precisely understands the workings of thought processes, what motivates people and demonstrates how, given that understanding, people can be cynically manipulated to fulfill the wishes of others.<br/><br/>Also the Vicomte de Valmont is a fantastic character whose ability to take revenge knows few, if any, equals (Edmond Dantes, perhaps).</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IM3co0/QyRHznRys/qiuOMD3U5OZADoAqwhSP3AbH0ca67Vx9I4AeVS2qM0JXaTnFqi0W1eNtGfwdhSOvVjt3Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_577246'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/577246.Manon_Lescaut?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Manon Lescaut<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/8563709.Antoine_Fran_ois_Pr_vost?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Antoine François Prévost<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.51 avg rating &mdash; 9,400 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1731\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer7912822891447066054\">\"\'The sweetness of her glance - or rather, my evil star already in its ascendant and drawing me to my ruin - did not allow me to hesitate for a moment...\'\" \"So begins the story of Manon Lescaut, a tale of passion and betrayal, of delinquency and misa<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7912822891447066054\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;\'The sweetness of her glance - or rather, my evil star already in its ascendant and drawing me to my ruin - did not allow me to hesitate for a moment...\'&quot; &quot;So begins the story of Manon Lescaut, a tale of passion and betrayal, of delinquency and misalliance, which moves from early eighteenth-century Paris - with its theatres, assemblies, and gaming-houses - via prison and deportation to a tragic denouement in the treeless wastes of Louisiana. It is one of the great love stories, and also one of the most enigmatic: how reliable a witness is Des Grieux, Manon\'s lover, whose tale he narrates? Is Manon a thief and a whore, the image of love itself, or a thoroughly modern woman? Prevost is careful to leave the ambiguities unresolved, and to lay bare the disorders of passion.&quot; This new translation includes the vignette and eight illustrations that were approved by Prevost Pour l\'écrire, l\'auteur s\'est documenté auprès du docteur Charcot, célèbre spécialiste des névroses et des hystéries. Mais surtout, il s\'est probablement inspiré des troubles nerveux qui commençaient alors à le détruire et qui allaient bientôt l\'empoter. Sans doute est-ce la raison pour laquelle cette histoire, qui évoque la possession de son unique personnage par une puissance invisible, est racontée sans aucun des artifices habituels de la fiction, mais avec une sécheresse presque clinique. Ce recueil contient une autre nouvelle fantastique. L\'Auberge, et des récits procédant de la veine réaliste habituelle de leur auteur, avec cet impitoyable mélange de cruauté psychologique et de pessimisme social qui caractérise l\'ensemble de son <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15212713600744188319">Pierre Ambroise François Choderlos de Laclos was a French novelist, official and army general, best known for writing the epistolary novel Les Liaisons dangereuses.<br/><br/>A unique case in French literature, he was for a long time considered to be as scandalous a writer as the Marquis de Sade or Nicolas-Edme Rétif. He was a military officer with no illusions about human relations, and an amateur writer; h</span>
  <span id="freeText15212713600744188319" style="display:none">Pierre Ambroise François Choderlos de Laclos was a French novelist, official and army general, best known for writing the epistolary novel Les Liaisons dangereuses.<br /><br />A unique case in French literature, he was for a long time considered to be as scandalous a writer as the Marquis de Sade or Nicolas-Edme Rétif. He was a military officer with no illusions about human relations, and an amateur writer; however, his initial plan was to "write a work which departed from the ordinary, which made a noise, and which would remain on earth after his death"; from this point of view he mostly attained his goals, with the fame of his masterwork Les Liaisons dangereuses . It is one of the masterpieces of novelistic literature of the 18th century, which explores the amorous intrigues of the aristocracy. It has inspired a large number of critical and analytic commentaries, plays, and films.<br /><br />Cyrillic profile: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7392092._________________" title="Шодерло дьо Лакло" rel="nofollow noopener">Шодерло дьо Лакло</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15212713600744188319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
